Home » FOI Request LEX5755

FOI Request LEX5755

Date: 2024

Summary of FOI Request Documents

Main Purpose of the FOI Request

The primary purpose of the FOI request appears to be to obtain internal documents of the Australian Electoral Commission (AEC) related to its procurement and contract management procedures, policies, and associated templates. The documents provided shed light on how the AEC manages its acquisition of goods and services, including detailed guidelines for staff and financial delegates.

Documents from the FOI Request

The released documents include a schedule of documents indicating the release of:
* AEC Intranet content related to Procurement and Contract Management.
* AEC Procurement and Contract Management Procedures (October 2020).
* Specific forms and templates:
* Procurement and Contract Risk Assessment
* Contract Management Checklist
* Contract Management Plan
* End of Contract Evaluation
* Procurement Variation Request Form
* Financial Delegate Checklist Variations
* Template – Work Order (specifically for DTA Digital Marketplace Specialist - Labour Hire)
* Internal policies and guidelines:
* Working Additional Hours Policy
* ETG Travel Guidelines

Many documents, particularly policy details and forms, contain redactions citing exemptions (e.g., Exemption 22, Exemption 47F).

Main Content from the FOI Request Documents

The released documents provide comprehensive insight into the AEC's internal governance regarding procurement and contracts:

1. AEC Procurement and Contract Management Procedures (October 2020)
* Purpose & Framework: Intends to guide AEC personnel on understanding and abiding by the Commonwealth’s procurement policy and framework, including the Public Governance, Performance and Accountability (PGPA) Act, Commonwealth Procurement Rules (CPRs), and specific AEC policies. Emphasizes achieving "Value for Money" (VFM) through efficient, effective, economical, and ethical use of public resources.
* Key Principles: Highlights accountability, transparency, competition, and non-discrimination as core principles.
* Procurement Process: Details a workflow from establishing business requirements, planning, market approach (open, limited, existing arrangements), evaluation, negotiation, to contract execution.
* Value Thresholds: Different rules and documentation requirements apply based on procurement value (e.g., over $80,000 for a business case, specific Quick Reference Guides for $80,000-$200,000 and over $200,000).
* Risk Management: Mandates identification, analysis, allocation, and treatment of risk throughout the procurement and contract lifecycle. Financial advice from the Financial Management Section is recommended for procurements valued at $200,000 and above.
* Indigenous Procurement Policy (IPP): Requires consideration of Indigenous Enterprises for services valued between $80,000 and $200,000, for Remote Procurements, and for high-value contracts in specific industries. AEC personnel must search Supply Nation.
* Existing Arrangements: Encourages leveraging existing Commonwealth or AEC panel arrangements ("piggybacking") to save time and expense, requiring a business case for justification if not using an open tender.
* Commonwealth Contracting Suite (CCS): Mandatory for procurements under $200,000 (with exemptions), aiming for consistency and streamlining.
* Record Keeping & Reporting: Mandates recording all procurement activities in My Procurement (AEC's system) and updating My Contracts throughout the agreement's life. Emphasizes transparency through AusTender reporting (e.g., contracts over $10,000, amendments, consultancies, high-value contracts twice yearly, Indigenous procurement details).
* Financial Delegates: Outlines responsibilities for delegates regarding committing public money (PGPA s23(3)), entering/varying arrangements (PGPA s23(1)), and granting indemnities/guarantees/warranties (PGPA s60), often requiring legal advice from Commercial Law and Procurement (CLP).
* Confidentiality & Unfair Contract Terms: Details protection of confidential information (applying a 'Confidentiality Test') and adherence to regulations against unfair contract terms, especially with small businesses.
* Ethics & Probity: Emphasizes ethical conduct, fairness, impartiality, conflict of interest management, and non-acceptance of gifts. Requires Probity Advisors for certain high-value or complex procurements.
* Political Neutrality: Requires suppliers to complete political neutrality declarations, with Financial Delegates considering potential risks to AEC's impartiality. Funding disclosure checks are conducted for suppliers.
* Consultancies: Provides criteria for identifying and reporting consultancy services in line with government requirements.
* Contingent Liabilities: Addresses obligations like indemnities, guarantees, or warranties, which require specific delegate approval and legal advice due to inherent risks.
* Black Economy Procurement Connected Policy (PCP): For contracts over $4 million, requires suppliers to provide a Statement of Tax Record (STR) from the Australian Taxation Office (ATO).

2. Forms and Templates (e.g., Contract Management Plan, Risk Assessment, Evaluation, Checklists, Work Order, Variation Request)
* These documents outline the structured approach the AEC takes at different stages of procurement and contract management. They serve as internal guides and accountability mechanisms.
* Contract Management Plan (CMP): Mandatory for significant election-related, high-value (>$80,000), or complex/high-risk contracts, focusing on performance monitoring, relationship management, and risk mitigation.
* Procurement and Contract Risk Assessment: Templates guide the systematic identification, analysis, and management of risks.
* End of Contract Evaluation: A template for assessing contract and supplier performance at completion, contributing to lessons learned.
* Financial Delegate Checklist Variations: Provides a detailed checklist for delegates to review proposed contract variations, ensuring compliance with VFM, policy, and legal considerations (e.g., IPP, Black Economy Policy, Political Neutrality).
* Template – Work Order: For labour hire, it specifies requirements such as police checks, security clearances, mandatory training, limitation of liability, WGE compliance, and Indigenous procurement. It also details terms for personnel replacement, termination conditions (including gross misconduct like unauthorized release of confidential material or fraud), and clauses for variations and work location. An attached Deed Poll covers confidentiality, privacy, and conflict of interest for individuals engaged.

3. ETG Travel Guidelines (6 December 2023)
* Purpose: Governs official travel for the Enterprise Transformation Group (ETG) staff.
* Core Principle: Adherence to "Lowest Practical Fare," prioritizing value for money over personal preference, loyalty points, or specific airlines/aircraft.
* Fare Class: Economy class is standard unless a specific business case or entitlement for business class is approved and documented.
* Booking Process:
* APS Staff: Book via Expense8, with prior APS Director (EL2+) approval. Reimbursement for eligible expenses (including personal card use for taxis/ride-shares) via Expense8.
* Labour-hire Staff: Travel booked by Program Support Team, requiring prior written approval from an APS Director (EL2+). Private vehicle use is a final contingency, requires higher-level approval, and is not covered by AEC insurance.
* Expense Claims (Labour-hire): Claims for taxis/ride-shares, travel time (1 hour pre-flight + flight duration), and itemized meal/incidental costs (up to specified daily rates: e.g., Breakfast $32.10, Lunch $36.10, Dinner $61.50, Incidentals $23.00, total $152.70/full day) are processed via their recruitment vendor against AEC Travel Policy. Alcoholic beverages are not reimbursed.
* Service Standards: Requests generally require 10 business days notice; urgent requests require additional approvals.

AI Generated ?

As a left-leaning analyst, the provided FOI documents from the Australian Electoral Commission (AEC) offer insights into government operations, particularly in procurement and contract management, revealing areas of both alignment with and deviation from progressive values.

Overall Progressive Lens: The documents showcase a governmental body attempting to adhere to financial probity and efficiency (Value for Money), while also incorporating some modern social policies. However, a critical analysis highlights areas where efficiency might overshadow broader social justice concerns, particularly regarding wealth distribution and civil liberties, and a notable absence concerning environmental impact.

1. Social Justice

  • Alignments:
    • Indigenous Procurement Policy (IPP): The AEC's commitment to the mandatory IPP (Page 53, "Procurement Procedures") is a positive step. It mandates considering Indigenous Enterprises for contracts between $80,000 and $200,000 and for remote procurements, leveraging government spending to support Indigenous economic empowerment.
    • Workplace Gender Equality (WGE): The requirement for contractors to provide an annual Workplace Gender Equality Agency compliance letter (Clause 29.1, Page 6, "Digital Marketplace Work Order") signals a commitment to gender equity within the supply chain, aligning with progressive values.
  • Deviations:
    • Labour-Hire Staff Conditions: The "ETG Travel Guidelines" expose a two-tiered system that disadvantages labour-hire staff compared to Australian Public Service (APS) staff (Pages 4-5).
      • Unequal Protections/Insurance: APS staff using private vehicles for official travel may be covered by AEC insurance for efficiencies, whereas labour-hire staff's private vehicles are explicitly "not covered by AEC insurance," requiring prior approval at a higher level as a "final contingency." This creates an unequal safety net.
      • Burdensome Reimbursement: While APS staff can use AEC credit cards or Expense8 for travel reimbursements, labour-hire staff must claim via their "recruitment vendor" by providing itemized receipts, potentially introducing delays and administrative burdens that impact their immediate financial liquidity.

2. Environmental Impact

  • Deviations/Omissions:
    • The documents notably lack explicit criteria or policies for environmental sustainability or impact within procurement decisions. While "market research" (Page 12, "Procurement Procedures") mentions exploring "sustainability options in the market," there's no mandate or strong emphasis on integrating environmental considerations into the core "Value for Money" (VFM) assessment (Page 9 and Appendix 4). This is a significant deviation from progressive values that prioritize ecological responsibility and climate action in government spending.

3. Wealth Distribution

  • Alignments:
    • Black Economy Procurement Connected Policy: The policy requiring tenders over $4 million to provide a satisfactory tax record (Appendix 16, Page 90, "Procurement Procedures") is a strong progressive alignment. It actively combats tax avoidance by large corporations, promoting fair wealth distribution and ensuring that public funds are not inadvertently supporting entities engaged in the black economy.
  • Deviations:
    • Reliance on Labour Hire & Worker Rights: The extensive framework for procurement and "recruitment and labour hire services" (Page 15, "Procurement Procedures") indicates a significant reliance on external staffing. This model often results in less secure employment, fewer benefits, and lower pay for workers compared to direct public sector employment, undermining progressive goals of robust worker protections and fair compensation.
    • Unfair Travel Time Compensation for Labour-Hire: A stark deviation is revealed in the "ETG Travel Guidelines" (Page 5), where labour-hire staff can only claim "airport-to-airport" travel time, calculated from one hour prior to flight to arrival. "Travel to and from each airport cannot be claimed." This is an extremely restrictive policy that effectively undercompensates labour-hire staff for their full work-related travel, amounting to a form of wage theft and exacerbating wealth inequality.
    • Limited Liability for Contractors: The "Digital Marketplace Work Order" (Clause 28.1, Page 6) explicitly limits the AEC's liability to the "Work Order price." While standard contractual practice, in the context of labour hire, this demonstrates the government's limited financial exposure to potential labor disputes or poor working conditions imposed by the private recruitment vendors, shifting risk away from the public body and onto the contracted entities and their workers.

4. Corporate Influence

  • Alignments:
    • Political Neutrality in Procurement: The "Political Neutrality" policy (Appendix 9, Page 73, "Procurement Procedures") mandates that Financial Delegates consider a supplier's political activity and any "real or perceived risk" to the AEC's political neutrality. This, coupled with "Funding disclosure checks," aims to prevent overt political influence or bias from shaping government contracts, which is a key progressive concern.
    • Transparency in Confidentiality: The policy's stance on supplier requests for confidentiality (Page 62, "Procurement Procedures") recommends pushing back against requests to keep "pricing information" confidential unless strict criteria are met. This supports transparency in public contracting, mitigating corporate power to hide financial details.
  • Potential Deviations:
    • Ambiguity of "Value for Money": While "Value for Money" (VFM) is the "core rule" of procurement (Page 9, "Procurement Procedures"), its broad definition, which considers factors beyond just price ("Value adds and benefits to the AEC"), could be interpreted to prioritize narrow financial gains over broader social or environmental benefits if not explicitly guided by progressive criteria. This ambiguity leaves room for corporate interests to align with cost-cutting at the expense of wider public good.

5. Civil Liberties

  • Potential Deviations:
    • Broad Security Checks & Political Affiliation Disclosure: The requirement for labour-hire personnel to have "no disclosable outcomes" from police checks (Clause 24.3, Page 6, "Digital Marketplace Work Order") and the AEC's right to conduct "any additional security checks" (Clause 24.4) are overly broad. Coupled with the "Conflict of Interest" definition in the "Deed Poll" (Page 12), which includes disclosing "any political affiliation that is affecting, will affect, or could be perceived to affect" performance, these policies could be seen as intrusive. While aiming for integrity, they risk infringing on privacy, freedom of association, and political expression, potentially creating a chilling effect for individuals seeking to work with the government. The lack of clear, narrow definitions for "disclosable outcomes" or "perceived conflict" allows for subjective interpretation and potential discrimination, deviating from robust civil liberties protections.

In conclusion, while the AEC documents demonstrate adherence to some progressive principles like supporting Indigenous businesses and gender equality, and actively combating tax evasion and overt political influence in contracting, they also reveal significant areas of concern. These include potentially exploitative labour-hire practices, a lack of explicit environmental commitment in procurement, and broad security/affiliation disclosure requirements that could impinge on civil liberties. A truly progressive approach would necessitate stronger mandates for social and environmental outcomes in VFM assessments, equitable treatment and full compensation for all workers, and narrower, more transparent criteria for personal background checks.

AI Generated ?

As a politically right-leaning analyst, the provided FOI documents from the Australian Electoral Commission (AEC) reveal a comprehensive, albeit potentially over-engineered, bureaucratic framework for procurement and contract management. While some elements align with conservative principles of fiscal responsibility and national security, significant deviations emerge concerning economic efficiency, individual liberty, and limited government, primarily due to expansive regulatory controls and intrusive oversight.

Economic Efficiency:
The documents strongly emphasize "Value for Money" (VFM) as the core procurement rule, seemingly aligning with efficient resource allocation. Procedures for competitive tenders, leveraging existing whole-of-government arrangements, and rigorous financial delegate checklists suggest an intent to optimize spending. Contract performance monitoring and evaluation also point towards a desire for effective outcomes. However, the broad definition of VFM, which explicitly includes "non-pricing considerations," creates ambiguity that could permit less economically efficient choices. The mandatory Indigenous Procurement Policy (IPP) set-asides, while aiming for social outcomes, inherently limit competition and may result in higher costs compared to a purely merit-based system. Furthermore, the sheer volume and granularity of procedural documentation, multi-layered approval processes (e.g., for limited tenders and variations), and complex reporting obligations impose significant administrative burdens, potentially driving up indirect costs and stifling agile decision-making, thus undermining true economic efficiency.

Individual Liberty:
The documents present notable deviations from individual liberty principles. The requirement for contractors and specified personnel to provide "political neutrality declarations" and for the AEC to conduct "funding disclosure checks" on a supplier's political activity (dating back three financial years) is a highly intrusive measure. This practice, linking professional engagement to political affiliation, could chill free association and speech, unfairly restricting market participation for otherwise qualified individuals and businesses. The designation of contractors' "hourly rate and associated fees" as confidential information, coupled with prohibitions on accessing other contractors' rates, limits transparency vital for individuals to negotiate freely and for a competitive labor market to function efficiently. Mandatory e-learning training requirements for contractors, while seemingly innocuous, contribute to an overarching tone of micromanagement over independent service providers.

National Security:
The documents demonstrate a clear alignment with national security concerns. Mandated police checks, additional security checks, security breach reporting protocols, and requirements for appropriate security clearances for personnel handling sensitive information are robust measures designed to protect the integrity and operational security of the AEC, particularly crucial in electoral processes.

Fiscal Responsibility:
The AEC's framework exhibits a strong commitment to fiscal responsibility through stringent financial delegation rules, an explicit focus on the "proper use of public resources" (efficient, effective, economical, and ethical), and detailed processes for spending proposals and contract variations. Travel guidelines emphasize "lowest practical fare," disallow frequent flyer point accrual, and impose caps on meal and incidental claims for labour-hire staff, all aimed at minimizing wasteful expenditure. However, the inherent complexity and layers of bureaucracy within the procurement system can lead to higher administrative overheads, indirectly straining fiscal resources. Furthermore, the policy of paying notice periods (up to 75 paid hours) even for buyer-initiated contract terminations that are not due to gross misconduct or essential qualification loss, could be viewed as fiscally irresponsible, as it may incentivize less rigorous performance management or premature termination.

Limited Government:
The documents reveal a significant expansion of government control beyond core functions into minute operational details. While comprehensive procedural documentation aims for structured accountability, its sheer volume and granular detail (e.g., prescribing specific communication plans, performance management mechanisms, and risk assessment templates for virtually all contracts) point to an over-regulated bureaucracy. The intrusive "political neutrality" checks and extensive reporting requirements on suppliers (even those with contracts under $10,000 using credit cards) extend the reach of government far beyond necessary oversight into the private affairs of citizens and businesses. The prevalence of "edited access" decisions in the FOI log also raises concerns about transparency, hindering public scrutiny and potentially allowing the government to operate with less accountability, contrary to the spirit of limited, transparent governance.

AI Generated ?

The provided FOI documents reveal an Australian Electoral Commission (AEC) operating with a concerning lack of transparency and an apparent bureaucratic over-reliance on internal processes that obscure public accountability, potentially leading to waste and questionable decision-making.

1. Pervasive Lack of Transparency and Redactions:
* Edited Access as the Default: The FOI decision log shows a near-universal application of "Edited access" for crucial documents, including "Procurement procedures and templates," "Contract management," "Procurement and Contract Risk Assessment," and even "Procurement Variation Request Forms." This widespread redaction, often citing exemptions like "22" and "47F" (likely commercial-in-confidence or internal deliberation), immediately suggests a deliberate effort to shield core operational details about how public money is spent and managed from public scrutiny.
* Confidentiality of Contractor Rates: The most glaring red flag is the explicit designation of a contractor's "hourly rate and associated fees" as "Confidential Information" in the Work Order template (Attachment A, clause 3.4). This directly prevents taxpayers from knowing what specific services cost, making it impossible to audit for inflated pricing, inconsistent rates, or preferential treatment. The same applies to "hourly rate and associated fees of other AEC contractors," which are also confidential (clause 3.5), fostering an opaque system ripe for inefficiencies and potential abuse.

2. Questionable Financial Management and Procurement Practices:
* "Value for Money" (VFM) Claims Undermined by Secrecy: While AEC procedures repeatedly stress "Value for Money" as the "core rule," the means by which VFM is assessed (e.g., "Procurement and Contract Risk Assessment") are largely hidden behind "Edited access." This creates a significant gap between proclaimed principle and demonstrable practice, inviting suspicion that VFM may be subjectively or leniently interpreted.
* Limited Tender Loopholes: Procedures allow "limited tenders" (approaching only one or a few suppliers) for procurements over $80,000, justifying this with an internal "business case." If these business cases are also hidden (as implied by the blanket redactions in the FOI log), it opens a clear avenue for non-competitive bidding, potentially leading to higher costs or favouritism without external oversight.
* "Piggybacking" Without Clear Value: The practice of "piggybacking" off existing arrangements (e.g., other agencies' contracts) is presented as efficient but is acknowledged to make it "very difficult to assess and demonstrate the achievement of value for money if only one Potential Supplier is approached." This admission highlights a systemic weakness where competitive pressures are bypassed, potentially enabling inflated prices under the guise of convenience.
* Indigenous Procurement Policy (IPP) Vulnerabilities: While laudable in intent, the use of "Exemption 17" for direct contracting with Indigenous Enterprises (between $80,000 and $200,000) without open tender raises concerns about competitive fairness. The reliance on internal satisfaction that a business is "at least 50% Indigenous owned" (page 55) could be exploited without robust, publicly verifiable due diligence.
* Blanket Approvals for Risk Acceptance: The Chief Legal Officer's "blanket approval" for PGPA Section 60 indemnities (page 33) in vehicle hire centralizes significant risk acceptance without granular, case-by-case public scrutiny. This could expose taxpayers to unforeseen liabilities under simplified internal processes.
* Discretion in "Black Economy" Compliance: The policy requiring a "satisfactory tax record" for contracts over $4 million includes a discretionary clause allowing the AEC to proceed with evaluation even if a supplier hasn't received their Statement of Tax Record (STR) by the submission date. This loophole could potentially allow non-compliant or preferred suppliers to slip through the net.

3. Weak Accountability and Oversight Mechanisms:
* Internal Performance Registers: The "Supplier Performance Register" (page 41), intended to "benchmark supplier performance," is located on an internal SharePoint site. The lack of public access to this crucial data means citizens cannot independently verify if poor-performing suppliers are re-engaged, or if the "lessons learned" touted in the documents actually translate into improved VFM.
* Limited Liability for Contractors: The Work Order template limits the seller's liability to the "maximum value of the Work Order price" (clause 28.1). This cap could disproportionately protect contractors in cases of severe negligence or significant damages, shifting the financial burden onto taxpayers if damages exceed the contract value.
* Consultancy Reporting Loopholes: The detailed flowchart for determining if a service is a "consultancy" (which triggers more reporting obligations) hints at a potential bureaucratic incentive to classify services as "non-consultancy" to reduce transparency.

4. Inefficient and Asymmetric Contractor Management:
* Two-Tiered Travel System: APS staff book their own travel directly, while labour-hire staff must use a "Program Support Team" via email and template. This adds administrative layers and potential delays for labour-hire, hinting at an inefficient bureaucracy that creates unnecessary hurdles.
* Disincentives for Cost-Effective Contractor Travel: Labour-hire staff are strongly discouraged from using private vehicles for official travel (only as "final contingency") and are explicitly not covered by AEC insurance, in contrast to APS staff. This likely forces reliance on more expensive options, driving up overall travel costs for the AEC.
* Short Notice Termination, Long Notice for Contractors: The AEC can terminate a Work Order with just "14 days (2 weeks) written notice," or "immediately" under certain conditions (clause 33.6). Conversely, the seller must provide "a minimum 42 days (6 weeks) written Notice" and "seek alternative options to termination" (clause 33.5). This highly asymmetric termination clause grants the AEC significant power, which could be used to prematurely end contracts for convenience, leaving contractors in a precarious position and potentially leading to higher pricing to offset such risk.

These FOI documents unequivocally highlight the government's unwavering commitment to effective, transparent, and ethical governance, ensuring public funds are managed with the utmost responsibility and integrity.

The detailed AEC Intranet – Procurement procedures and templates (Document 3) and the comprehensive AEC Procurement and Contract Management Procedures (Document 5) showcase a robust framework meticulously designed to achieve 'Value for Money' (VFM) – the cornerstone of responsible spending. Every step, from identifying a business need to contract execution, is clearly defined, emphasizing compliance with the Public Governance, Performance and Accountability (PGPA) Act and Commonwealth Procurement Rules (CPRs). This structured approach, supported by clear financial delegation and approval processes (Document 5, Appendix 6), ensures accountability and the ethical use of taxpayer money.

The proactive approach to managing public funds is further demonstrated by the detailed AEC Intranet - Contract management (Document 4) and supporting tools like the Procurement and Contract Risk Assessment (Document 6) and the Contract Management Plan (Document 8). These documents underscore a strategic focus on monitoring performance, fostering professional relationships with suppliers, and identifying and mitigating potential risks early. The use of structured guidance like the Contract Management Checklist (Document 7) ensures thoroughness and consistency in oversight, directly maximizing outcomes for the public.

Challenges, where they arise, are met with robust, pre-defined procedures. The detailed sections on ethics, probity, and political neutrality (Document 5, Appendix 8, Appendix 9) within the procurement procedures illustrate the government’s dedication to fairness, impartiality, and maintaining public trust, particularly for a politically neutral body like the AEC. Strict communication protocols, the rejection of late submissions, and mandatory financial viability assessments for tenderers reinforce a level playing field. The specific focus on the Indigenous Procurement Policy (Document 5, Appendix 3) also reflects the government's commitment to leveraging procurement for broader societal benefits.

Even routine operational aspects are subject to rigorous standards, as seen in the ETG Travel Guidelines (Document 10). These guidelines enforce principles of 'Lowest Practical Fare' and 'Necessity of Travel,' ensuring that travel expenses are managed judiciously and efficiently, further demonstrating fiscal prudence.

Finally, the mandatory End of Contract Evaluation (Document 9) and continuous reporting obligations, including publishing agreements on AusTender (Document 5, Appendix 5), underscore a dedication to continuous improvement and transparency. By systematically learning from past experiences and making public information accessible, the government consistently refines its processes to deliver even more effective and efficient public services. This holistic approach ensures comprehensive oversight and an unwavering commitment to serving the public interest.

The following summary ruthlessly dissects the provided FOI documents, highlighting every negative implication and presenting the most damning possible interpretation of the government's actions and inactions.

The FOI documents from the Australian Electoral Commission (AEC) reveal a staggering and deliberate culture of opacity, systemic incompetence, and a high probability of unchecked financial mismanagement and potential corruption. Far from promoting accountability, these heavily redacted internal documents serve as a cynical testament to the AEC's active efforts to conceal its operational failings and shield itself from public scrutiny.

1. Blatant Obstruction and Institutional Secrecy:
The most damning revelation is the wholesale redaction of nearly all substantive content across every document. Core internal guidelines such as "AEC Intranet – Procurement procedures and templates," "AEC Intranet - Contract management," "AEC Procurement and Contract Management Procedures - October 2020," "Procurement and Contract Risk Assessment," "Contract Management Checklist," "Contract Management Plan," and "End of Contract Evaluation" are almost entirely blacked out using "Exemption 22" (confidentiality) and "47F" (deliberative process). This is not "edited access"; it is a complete and utter stonewall. The fact that even templates for managing contracts and evaluating performance are hidden suggests that the AEC is actively suppressing critical information that would expose its operational weaknesses, financial vulnerabilities, or even malfeasance. The token release of a single, utterly trivial "Financial Delegate Checklist Variations" in full mocks the very principle of transparency.

2. Systemic Loopholes and Financial Recklessness:
The unredacted fragments expose a procurement framework riddled with gaping loopholes, designed to facilitate unchecked spending and evade accountability:
* The "$25,000 / $80,000 Corporate Credit Card Black Hole": The AEC explicitly states that procurements under $25,000 (later revised to under $80,000) paid by corporate credit card are not recorded in the main "FIMS" or "My Procurement" systems. This is a deliberate, massive accountability bypass, creating a sprawling black economy within the AEC where potentially millions of dollars in public funds can be spent on untraceable goods and services without any centralized oversight or formal record. This invites rampant fraud, personal enrichment, and a complete breakdown of financial control.
* Verbal Approvals for Travel: Shockingly, "the need for travel must be approved by an APS Director (EL2 or above) verbally or in writing prior to booking travel." Verbal approvals for significant travel expenses are an open invitation to abuse, lacking any auditable paper trail and allowing directors to bypass formal processes at will.
* Uninsured Private Vehicles for Official Travel: In an act of morally bankrupt financial corner-cutting, the AEC permits labour-hire staff to use their uninsured private vehicles for official travel as a "final contingency." This policy exposes individuals to massive personal risk for the AEC's convenience, highlighting a callous disregard for worker safety and a desperate attempt to avoid legitimate travel costs.
* Blanket Approval for Contingent Liabilities: The Chief Legal Officer provides "blanket approval" for accepting financial indemnities in vehicle hire arrangements, bypassing case-by-case scrutiny. Even more egregiously, the AEC knowingly accepts potential liabilities between $5 million and $30 million that their own Comcover insurance "normally will not pay," only requiring that CLP be "notified." This is an astonishing level of financial recklessness, exposing the public to massive, uninsured losses.

3. Incompetence Masquerading as Policy:
The documents reveal a deep-seated institutional incompetence and a reactive, rather than proactive, approach to critical functions:
* Weak Risk Management: Reliance on "standard risk templates" and optional financial advice for large procurements (only "recommended" for $200,000+ contracts) suggests a tick-box approach to risk assessment. The redaction of the actual risk matrix and criteria further confirms that their proclaimed risk management is a charade.
* Illusion of Value for Money: The repeated platitudes about achieving "Value for Money" (VFM) are rendered meaningless by the pervasive redactions. How can VFM be demonstrated if the underlying documentation and evaluation criteria are hidden? The admission that VFM is "very difficult to assess" when only one supplier is approached for a contract (yet this is allowed) further exposes the hypocrisy.
* Lack of Performance Enforcement: The shocking admission of "no set procedure for managing under performance" means the AEC is ill-equipped to address supplier failures, allowing poor performance to fester. The casual directive that performance issues "should must be recorded" further underscores the bureaucratic sloppiness.
* Pervasive "Tips" and "Recommendations": The frequent use of "Tips," "Recommendations," and "may assist" language for critical procedures (like avoiding unfair competitive advantages, seeking financial advice, or using performance incentives) suggests that fundamental best practices are optional or inconsistently applied, rather than mandatory.
* Internal Communication Failures: The "AEC Escalation Framework" highlights that contract managers routinely face issues "above their level of authority" and must rely on informal "relationships" to resolve problems, indicating systemic internal communication breakdowns and a preference for informal problem-solving over auditable processes. The scenarios provided confirm repeated failures in basic service delivery (damaged furniture, poor cleaning) requiring high-level intervention.

4. Ethical Lapses and Conflicts of Interest:
The documents inadvertently highlight a troubling awareness of ethical vulnerabilities:
* Warnings of "Too Intimate" Relationships: The explicit warning against relationships with suppliers becoming "too intimate" strongly suggests that such ethical breaches, potentially involving favoritism or corruption, are a known and ongoing problem within the AEC.
* Political Neutrality as a Condition, Not a Pre-Requisite: The AEC "considers" a supplier's political activity and may grant approval on a "conditional basis" with "identified control measures." This allows politically connected entities to gain contracts with hidden, potentially superficial, "mitigation" measures. The exemption of contracts under $10,000 from political activity checks, and the lack of re-checks for repeat suppliers within six months, creates a clear avenue for subverting political neutrality through fragmented or recurring small contracts.
* Prohibition Implies Past Misconduct: Explicit prohibitions against accruing frequent flyer points, upgrading fares, claiming alcohol expenses, or misusing corporate credit cards for ride-sharing imply that these unethical practices were, or are, prevalent enough to necessitate specific rules.
* Self-Certifying Indigenous Businesses: The AEC can use "Exemption 17" to contract with Indigenous Enterprises regardless of their Supply Nation registration, allowing them to self-certify Indigenous status without independent verification. This is a massive loophole prone to exploitation, potentially funneling public money to non-Indigenous entities masquerading as such.

5. Intentional Opacity as Standard Operating Procedure:
The sheer volume of redactions across critical documents like the "Procurement and Contract Risk Assessment" and the "Contract Management Plan" is not merely administrative; it is a calculated act of institutional self-protection. The complete suppression of "Lessons Learnt and Improvements" from public view is the ultimate cover-up, indicating either an absence of genuine learning or lessons so damning they cannot be revealed.

In conclusion, these FOI documents do not demonstrate a transparent and accountable public service. Instead, they expose a deeply flawed and deliberately opaque system of procurement and contract management, characterized by a lack of oversight, inconsistent application of rules, high financial risks, and a pervasive culture of secrecy that actively prevents public scrutiny and accountability. The AEC's actions, or inactions, present a grave threat to the integrity of public spending and public trust.

2. 

Access  

2.1  The Confidant understands that the Work Order may contain Confidential information and in the course of

3. 

Non-disclosure 

3.1  The Confidant will treat as secret and confidential all Personal Information and Confidential Information to which 

it has access or which is disclosed to the Confidant.  

3.2 

If the AEC grants its consent for the Confidant to disclose Personal Information or Confidential Information, it 
may impose conditions on that consent. In particular, the AEC may require that the Confidant obtains the 
execution of a deed in these terms by the person to whom the Confidant proposes to disclose the Personal 
Information or Confidential Information. 

3.3  The Confidant’s obligations under this deed will not be taken to have been breached where the Confidant is 

legally required to disclose the Personal Information or Confidential Information. 

3.4  For the avoidance of doubt, the Confidant’s hourly rate and associated fees is designated as Confidential 
Information for the purposes of this Deed. The Confidant must not disclose this Confidential Information to 
anyone other than their employer and the Buyer representative. 

3.5  The hourly rate and associated fees of other AEC contractors is also designated Confidential Information. The 
Confidant must not access this Confidential Information from AEC IT systems without the express written 
permission of the Buyer representative and only for a purpose that is directly relevant to the performance of the 
Services. 

4. 

Restriction on use 

4.1  The Confidant will use the Personal Information or Confidential Information only for the purpose of its dealings 

with the AEC (whether directly or indirectly). 

4.2  The Confidant will not copy, reproduce or disclose the Personal Information or Confidential Information without 
the approval of the AEC, will not allow any other person outside the AEC access to the Personal Information or 
Confidential Information, except to employees of the Service Provider who have a need to know basis for such 
information and, for security classified information, have the appropriate security clearance and will take all 
necessary precautions to prevent unauthorised access to or copying or disclosure of the Personal Information 
or Confidential Information in its control. 

4.3  Where Personal Information is contained within AEC IT systems, the Confident agrees not to disclose that 

Personal Information outside of the AEC IT systems, including via email to an external email address, without 
the express written consent of the Buyer representative. 

4.4  The Confidant will ensure that anybody to whom it provides or discloses the Personal Information or 

Confidential Information is made aware of the conditions under which this information is provided or disclosed, 
and of the fact that the confidentiality/secrecy of the information must be maintained.  

4.5  Where the Confidant is an individual it will undergo all relevant security clearance vetting processes where it 

has, or will be given, access to security classified information for more than three months.

--- Page 1 ---

6 December 2023 

Enterprise Transformation 
Group Travel Guideline 

Audience: Enterprise Transformation Group (ETG) 

Classification: OFFICIAL


--- Page 2 ---




--- Page 3 ---

Purpose 

The purpose of this document is to outline the processes that ETG staff need to abide by and follow 
when arranging, booking or undertaking official AEC travel. 

Guidelines  

When undertaking official travel, AEC staff must select the Lowest Practical Fare, which is the lowest 
fare available at the time that travel is booked, that suits the practical business needs of the traveller. 
AEC officials are required to abide by the following guidelines from the Resource Management Guide 
No. 404, Official Domestic Travel – Use of the lowest practical fare: 

•  Value for Money: Value for money is the overarching consideration when booking flights for 
domestic air travel. Value for money requires the use of Commonwealth resources in an 
efficient, effective, economical, and ethical manner that is not inconsistent with the policies of 
the Commonwealth. Accordingly, when booking travel, officials must make decisions based 
on an impartial consideration of the fares available and not on a personal preference for a 
particular airline or aircraft type, access to airline lounges or accumulating airline reward and 
loyalty points (including status credits). AEC staff cannot accrue frequent flyer points whilst on 
official travel, upgrade fares, or select specific airlines on the basis of memberships held. 

•  Necessity of Travel: Air travel must only be undertaken where other communication tools, 
such as teleconferencing and videoconferencing, are ineffective. In approving travel, the 
delegate (the official approving domestic air travel) must be satisfied that there is a 
demonstrated business need for the travel.  

•  Diligence: AEC staff must act in accordance with the AEC Travel Policy and the Australian 
Public Service Code of Conduct. This includes acting reasonably in scheduling meetings or 
other events for which travel is considered necessary (e.g. minimising unnecessary travel 
time). 

AEC officials must make two key decisions when selecting a fare for travel:  

•  Fare class: All air travel must be the lowest practical fare in economy class unless there is a 
business case or entitlement to travel business class. If the relevant delegate approves 
business class travel where there is no entitlement, the reasons for travel in that class must 
be documented in each instance.  

•  Fare type: Where there is a high degree of certainty for required arrival or departure times, 
officials must assess all fares and consider restricted fare types. Where there is a possibility 
that a scheduled meeting will not proceed, or there is uncertainty around the time that a 
scheduled meeting may conclude, officials must consider whether the additional cost of 
flexible fares outweighs the cost of possible changes or cancellation fees. 

Arranging and Booking Travel 

The process for booking and arranging travel, as well as seeking reimbursement of incidental costs 
associated with travel, for labour-hire staff is similar to the process in place for APS staff with a few 
variations. 

Updated as of 6 December 2023


--- Page 4 ---

APS Staff 

•  APS Staff book their own flights, accommodation and hire car (where needed) through 

Expense8 and must adhere to the Whole of Australian Government Travel Arrangements and 
the AEC Travel Policy. 

•  The need for travel must be approved by an APS Director (EL2 or above) verbally or in writing 
prior to booking travel. This is due to the financial and resourcing delegations that exist only 
within these positions. The Expense8 booking should then be submitted to the appropriate 
director for final approval. 

•  APS staff may only be granted approval to use a private motor vehicle for official purposes 
where it will result in greater efficiencies and will not involve unnecessary expenses for the 
AEC. Refer to the AEC Travel Policy for further details. 

•  APS staff will be provided all eligible entitlements through Expense8 automatically once their 
booking has been approved by the delegate. Entitlements will be processed through the AEC 
Financial Management System into their nominated bank account.  

•  APS staff must adhere to the Travel Policy and the Enterprise Agreement for their 

entitlements regarding travel time. 

•  APS staff travelling by taxi are to pay for their fare with an AEC credit card and retain the 

receipt. If the traveller does not have an AEC credit card, they can use a personal card and 
the cost will be reimbursed by submitting a request in Expense8 with the receipt attached. 
Labour-hire staff travelling with APS Staff should share taxis where possible. 

•  APS staff may access ride-sharing services when on official travel and where they are legal. 
AEC staff must not use their AEC credit card to pay for these services (or store the card 
details in their ride-share app). The traveller can use a personal card and the cost will be 
reimbursed by submitting a request in Expense8 with the receipt attached. 

Labour-hire Staff 

•  Flights, accommodation and hire car (where needed) will be booked by the Program Support 

Team for labour-hire staff in line with the Whole of Australian Government Travel 
Arrangements and the AEC Travel Policy. 

•  The need for travel must be approved by an APS Director (EL2 or above) in writing prior to 
booking travel. This is due to the financial and resourcing delegations that exist only within 
these positions. This written approval must be attached to the Expense8 travel booking when 
it is submitted for final approval. 

•  Labour-hire staff should only consider the use of a private vehicle for travel as a final 

contingency where flights and hire car arrangements are not feasible. This kind of travel 
requires prior approval in writing by an AEC Assistant Commissioner, the Labour-Hire Staff’s 
Employment Vendor and Program Support. In these cases, the vehicle will not be covered by 
AEC insurance as per the AEC Travel Policy. 

•  As labour-hire staff do not hold Expense8 profiles, travel booking requests are made via the 

Travel Booking Template Form.   

•  The Travel Booking Form must be sent by email to 

 with proof of the 

travel approval from an APS Manager. 

•  The AEC will complete the booking and send the travel documents by email to the traveller 

once finalised. 

•  All required Taxi or Ride-share costs between Home-Airport-Accommodation-Workplace 

during work-related travel should be claimed via the labour-hire staff member’s recruitment 

Updated as of 6 December 2023 

s22


--- Page 5 ---

vendor by providing receipts in the first instance and will be paid in line with the AEC Travel 
Policy. 

•  Labour-hire staff can claim travel time (airport-to-airport). This will be calculated from one hour 
prior to the flight time until the flight arrival time at the destination. Travel to and from each 
airport cannot be claimed.  

•  All meal and incidental costs incurred by labour-hire staff for work-related travel should be 

claimed via the labour-hire staff member’s recruitment vendor by providing itemised receipts. 
Meals and incidentals can be claimed of actual costs for the amount spent, up to the rates 
below: 

Breakfast 

$32.10 

Lunch 

Dinner 

$36.10 

$61.50 

Incidentals 

$23.00 

•  Reimbursement claims must be approved by an AEC delegate and included in a correctly 

rendered invoice along with all relevant itemised receipts to substantiate any claims. The total 
amount claimable per day is $152.70 for each full day worked; the amount is pro-rated for 
part-days.  

•  The AEC does not reimburse any claims for purchases of alcoholic beverages.  

Service Standards 

Requests to book travel must be sent to Indigo Support at least 10 business days prior to the travel 
departure date, where possible.  

If travel is required within 10 business days, or the request is urgent, please email 

copying in the Program Coordinator and the Program Support Director and 

mark your email as urgent. The relevant Assistant Commissioner will be required to approve this 
urgent travel request. 

More information can be found on the AEC travel intranet site at

1.  Introduction 

Scope 
The Procurement and Contract Management Procedures (the Procedures) are intended to allow AEC 
personnel to better understand the Commonwealth’s procurement policy and framework. The Procedures 
emphasise the responsibilities of AEC personnel, and the AEC’s expectations in undertaking 
procurements of any value on behalf of the AEC and the Australian Government. 

AEC personnel who are required to spend public money of any value or enter into an Agreement must 
comply with the Commonwealth Resource Management Framework. 

In these Procedures all monetary figures are inclusive of GST, unless otherwise specified. 

Commonwealth Resource Management Framework 
AEC personnel must procure Services in accordance with the: 

(a)  Public Governance, Performance and Accountability Act 2013 (PGPA Act), 

(b)  Public Governance, Performance and Accountability Rules 2014 (PGPA Rules),  

(c)  Commonwealth procurement framework, including: 

(i) 

the Commonwealth Procurement Rules (CPRs);  

(ii)  Resource Management Guides; 

(iii) 

templates such as the Commonwealth Contracting Suite (CCS); and 

(iv) 

the Department of Finance’s procurement policy website and guidance,  

(d)  Commonwealth Indigenous Procurement Policy (IPP); and 

(e)  AEC internal procedures and operational guidelines, including: 

(i) 

the Accountable Authority Instructions (AAIs), specifically AAIs (Part 2); and 

(ii) 

these Procedures. 

How to use these Procedures 
The Procedures provide information and processes for AEC personnel completing procurement and 
contract management activities. Additional information on key areas of the Procedures are included in 
the Appendices. 

Exclusions 
Travel arrangements for AEC personnel are only considered in these Procedures to the extent that the 
arrangements require a legal review (e.g. Contingent Liabilities). Contact AEC Travel for further 
information on arranging travel.  

Grants are not included in these Procedures. 

Resources and Templates 
Relevant links referenced in the Procedures are as follows:

3.  Procurement 

Overview 
Procurement encompasses the whole process of procuring goods and services, beginning when a need 
has been identified and a decision has been made on the procurement requirement. 

At a high level a procurement activity follows the below process flow: 

Establish business 
requirement(s)

Retrieve approval to 
proceed (Business 
case or other)

Plan the 
procurement

Consider probity 
obligations

Draft Approach to 
Market (ATM) 
documentation

Retrieve approval for 
ATM

Release ATM

Evaluate responses 
to ATM

Document evaluation 
process and outcome

Retrieve spending 
approval

Negotiate contract

Execute contract

When taking part in a procurement activity, AEC personnel and AEC Financial Delegates must:  

(a)  act in accordance with legislation, policy and procedural requirements; 

(b)  act within appropriation, authorisation or delegation;  

(c) 

act ethically, fairly and impartially; 

(d)  achieve VFM at all times; 

(e)  act in an open, accountable and transparent manner; and 

(f) 

encourage competition and non-discrimination. 

Irrespective of the size or nature of any procurement, AEC personnel must seek to achieve VFM in 
accordance with the CPRs to ensure AEC Financial Delegates are satisfied that a VFM outcome has 
been reached. 

When a business requirement arises, AEC personnel should consider how a procurement will deliver 
the best VFM outcome. This should begin by clearly understanding and expressing the goals and 
purpose of the procurement, and must include consideration to leverage any existing AEC 
arrangements. 

AEC personnel must exercise caution to ensure they do not enter into an Agreement orally (or give the 
perception of) or by the exchange of non-contractual documents, letters or emails. Unknowingly 
entering into an Agreement can occur, for example, where a AEC personnel instruct a Supplier to 
proceed with the Services before the commencement date of an Agreement. 

AEC Procurement and Contract Management Procedures 

Page 8 of 90


--- Page 9 ---

Important Note: VFM and proper use of public resources 

Achieving  VFM  is  the  core  rule  of  the  CPRs.  VFM  is  not  determined  by  which  response  is  the 
cheapest.  Dollar  value  is  just  one  component  of  an  overall  VFM  assessment.  All  of  the  VFM 
considerations (including non-pricing considerations) must be analysed, all decisions/outcomes are 
to be well substantiated and fully documented to establish a transparent and defensible VFM outcome 
for the AEC.  

Notable considerations for the VFM assessment are: 

  Consideration of full tendered contract value, including whole-of-life costs, 
  Comparison  of  the  tendered  contract  value  to  the  estimated  contract  value  as  per  the 

procurement plan, 

  Competition noted within the procurement process, including:  

o  how many submissions were received, 
o  analysis of trends in pricing submissions; and 
o  negotiations undertaken to reduce tendered values. 

  Value adds and benefits to the AEC, 
  Document any changes in scope from initial estimate,  
  Any clarification undertaken to ensure consistency in respondent approach; and 
  Risk exposure to the AEC presented in submissions. 

AEC personnel responsible for a procurement must be satisfied, after reasonable enquiries, that the 
procurement achieves a VFM outcome. Refer CPRs Division 1: Rules for all procurements. 

VFM should always be considered throughout the entire procurement process, including when looking 
to extend or vary a contract that increases the contract value. 

AEC Delegates are responsible for ensuring that the commitment of relevant money is a proper use of 
public resources - efficient, effective, economical and ethical - that is consistent with the policies of the 
Commonwealth. AEC Delegates must assure themselves that a procurement activity has: 

facilitates accountable and transparent decision making,  

  encouraged competition and is non-discriminatory, 
 
  encourages appropriate engagement and management of risk; and  
 

is commensurate with the scale and scope of the business requirement. 

Procurement workflow 
The following flowchart depicts the basic workflow for a procurement process. However, tasks required 
to comply with the CPRs will vary depending on the procurement method, value, complexity and risk, 
and as such these Procedures must be read as a whole.

Do other business areas have the same or similar requirements within 
the AEC? 

AEC personnel must understand what the market for the Services required is within the AEC: 

(a)  Do other business areas require similar or the same Services? 

(b)  Does another business area already have an Agreement in place for the Services? 

(c) 

Is there a sufficient requirement in the AEC to establish a panel arrangement for those Services? 

AEC personnel should also look at the Election Procurement Strategy to answer these questions. 

What does the supply market dictate about the requirements? 

To fully understand the market associated with a procurement AEC personnel should conduct market 
research to a scale appropriate to the procurement activity as a whole. For the purposes of 
procurement, market research is the act of gathering, analysing and interpreting information about 
the particular market in which the AEC is proposing to conduct a procurement. 

Market research can provide a strategic understanding of: 

(a)  how a market works, 

(b) 

the direction in which a market is moving (e.g. technological advancement), 

(c) 

the competitiveness of a market, 

(d) 

key suppliers, 

(e) 

sustainability options in the market; and 

(f) 

how to manage price variations over time or between suppliers. 

Methods for conducting market research include: 

(a)  online research,  

(b) 

contacting other Australian Government agencies, 

(c) 

consulting subject matter experts, 

(d)  phone surveys, 

(e) 

visits to local potential supplier, 

(f) 

conducting workshops; and 

(g) 

conducting a request for expression of interest (EOI) or request for information (RFI).  

Tip: Care must be taken to avoid the perception that any Potential Supplier has received information 
that provides them with an unfair competitive advantage, or the perception that work has been 
promised. Additional information on market research can be found in Appendix 1 of these Procedures. 

What are the procurement risks? 

AEC personnel must identify, analyse, allocate and treat risk throughout the entire procurement and 
contract management process. 

Depending on an initial assessment and value, a full risk assessment may be required. 

Additional information on Procurement and Contract Risk can be found in Appendix 2 of these 
Procedures. 

As part of managing risk, the AEC Financial Delegate may require additional assurance by way of a 
financial analysis of the procurement and/or any Potential Suppliers. For any procurements valued at 
$200k and above, it is recommended that business areas seek financial advice from the Financial 

AEC Procurement and Contract Management Procedures 

Page 12 of 90


--- Page 13 ---




--- Page 14 ---

Open tenders 

An open tender involves publishing a Request for Tender (RFT) to the market and inviting 
Submissions from any interested Suppliers via the AusTender website. 

Limited tenders 

Limited tenders involve approaching one or more Potential Suppliers to make Submissions when the 
procurement process does not meet the rules for open tenders. 

For procurements valued at or above the threshold of $80 000, a limited tender can only be conducted 
in accordance with paragraph 10.3 of the CPRs or when a procurement is exempt, as detailed in 
Appendix A of the CPRs, as supported by a business case, approved by the AEC Financial Delegate 
and attached to the Approach to Market in My Procurement. 

AEC personnel must contact CLP if considering undertaking a limited tender valued at or above $80, 
000. 

Is there an Indigenous enterprise that I can approach? 

The Indigenous Procurement Policy (IPP) mandates that Indigenous Enterprises be considered for the 
delivery of Services valued between $80,000 and $200,000, on a value for money basis prior to 
approaching the market. 

Additional information on the Indigenous Procurement Policy can be found in Appendix 3 of these 
Procedures. 

Are there any existing arrangements that can meet the AEC’s business 
requirements? 

Accessing Services through Existing Arrangements established by the AEC or other Commonwealth 
agencies (often referred to as ‘piggybacking’) may save the AEC time, resources and expense in 
conducting separate procurement processes. It can also enable the AEC to access more favourable 
rates and conditions that other larger agencies were able to negotiate. 

Procurements from Existing Arrangements are only subject to the rules in Division 1 of the CPRs. 
However, the establishment of an Existing Arrangement is subject to both Division 1 and 2 of the 
CPRs. 

Where there is an Existing Arrangement available to the AEC, with a scope that clearly meets the 
AEC’s requirements, AEC personnel may use that Arrangement. 

Important: If you intend on accessing an Existing Arrangement instead of completing an Open 
Tender procurement process, a business case, approved by the AEC Financial Delegate and 
attached to the Approach to Market in My Procurement, is required to outline the decision making 
process. The business case must include the following information at a minimum: 

  why the Arrangement is being accessed,  
  how the scope of the Arrangement meets the AEC’s business needs,  
  what Potential Suppliers will be approached; and  
  why you have selected those Potential Suppliers. 

Important: IF you intend on accessing an Existing Arrangement, AEC Contact Officers should 
consider how many Potential Suppliers are to be approached. Note that it will be very difficult to 
assess and demonstrate the achievement of value for money if only one Potential Supplier is 
approached. 

AEC Procurement and Contract Management Procedures 

Page 14 of 90


--- Page 15 ---

Existing Arrangements may include: 

(a)    Whole of Australian Government (WoAG) arrangements 

These are Arrangements, some of which are mandated, established by the Department of 
Finance to benefit Commonwealth agencies for the procurement of Services. 

The Department of Finance procurement website provides the full consolidated list of these 
arrangements. When purchasing from an Arrangement, AEC personnel must ensure that the: 

(i) 

Services required are within the scope of the Arrangement; 

(ii) 

terms and conditions of the Arrangement are not being materially altered; and 

(iii) 

relevant templates are used to procure the Services. 

An Agreement is formed under an Arrangement when a Work Order is issued, or executed by 
both parties, as specified in the Head Agreement or Deed of Standing Offer. The Work Order 
together with the Head Agreement or Deed of Standing Offer form the Agreement. 

(b)    AEC National Panels 

The AEC has established National Panels for the provision of furniture and equipment, logistics 
and ballot material printing to ensure nationally consistent arrangements for key election 
services. 

Refer to the Election Procurement Strategy intranet page for guidance on procuring under these 
Arrangements. 

(b)    Recruitment and labour hire services 

National Work Orders (WOs) are in place with six (6) providers for the provision of 
recruitment and related services (excludes ICT recruitment). These WOs can be used by any 
business area within the AEC to fulfil recruitment and labour hire needs for both BAU and 
elections (as a contingency only to fulfil larger TEW requirements). 

It is not mandatory to use these WOs but it is considered to be good practice should they 
meet your business requirements. 

These WOs do not replace any existing contracts for recruitment services. 

These WOs can be accessed using a simple 5-step process. Guidance on how to use these 
arrangements includes FAQs and applicable templates, as well as a capability matrix that 
provides contact details of providers and their service capabilities and jurisdictions. This 
document, as well as other relevant documents, are available through the CLP’s SharePoint 
site. AEC staff need permission to access this site. Submit a request to access the site by 
contacting Commercial Law and Procurement. 

 (c)    Panel arrangements 

A panel arrangement (also known as Standing Offer Panel) is the end result of a procurement 
process, where a number of Suppliers are appointed through a head agreement or deed of 
standing offer. 

A list of Existing (Panel) Arrangements that the AEC has access to can be found on the CLP’s 
SharePoint site.  AEC personnel wishing to utilise one of these Existing Arrangements should 
first contact the relevant AEC Panel Manager to obtain general information and guidance on 
approaching the Existing Arrangement. Existing Arrangement documentations i.e. Agreements, 
templates and related documents can be found on DS4P. If an AEC Official requires access to 
DS4P they must contact CLP.

7.  Vehicle hire 

Whole of Australia Government arrangement (WoAG) 
All vehicle hires within Australia must be made through the WoAG contracted provider, Hertz.  

If vehicle hire is being arranged with domestic flights, it can be booked through the Online Booking Tool.  

If you are only hiring a vehicle, you can book direct with Hertz, online or by telephone. The terms and 
conditions for hiring vehicles from Hertz are contained in the Deed between the Department of Finance 
and Hertz.  

Upon collection of a vehicle from Hertz, the AEC official may be required to sign a document. The 
purpose of this document is to identify the Authorised Driver. Clause 14.2 of the Deed that the 
Department of Finance has in place with Hertz confirms this by stating that the Deed prevails over any 
other rental agreements, provisions or terms. Accordingly, the legal risk to the AEC is low if an AEC 
official is required to sign other terms and conditions as the terms of the Deed can be relied on. To limit 
the risk further however, we recommend resisting the requirement to sign any other document. 

More information about vehicle hire under the WoAG arrangement can be found on the Department of 
Finance's website. In particular you may find the Department of Finance's FAQ section useful. 

General AEC procedures for hiring vehicles are contained in the AEC's Travel Procedures.  

Alternative providers 
Where a Hertz rental office is not within reasonable proximity, or no vehicles are available, AEC personnel 
may use an alternative provider that represents best value for money.  

In such instances, AEC personnel must: 

a.  seek Commercial Law and Procurement section advice on the alternative provider's terms and 

conditions before you enter into any agreement; and 

b.  advise AEC Travel in order that it can be reported to Department of Finance for contract review 

and compliance purposes.  

To help make this process quicker for business areas, legal advice has been provided below for 
alternative providers that the AEC has previously used. If a business area uses a provider on a regular 
basis and they are not listed on the Vehicle Hire intranet page, please provide the terms and conditions to 
CLP for review as soon as possible. Further, if a business area becomes aware that a provider listed on 
the Vehicle Hire intranet page has changed their terms and conditions, please provide the updated terms 
and conditions to CLP for review as soon as possible. 

Indemnities 
MOST vehicle hire arrangements, including those under the WoAG arrangement, require the AEC to 
indemnify the provider. Under section 60 of the Public Governance, Performance and Accountability 
(PGPA) Act 2013, the AEC cannot grant an indemnity in any arrangement unless there is delegate 
approval.  

PGPA Section 60 Blanket Approval and Risk Assessment 
To simplify the PGPA section 60 approval process and support compliance, the Chief Legal Officer has 
provided blanket approval based on a standard risk assessment for vehicle hire which can be found on 
the Vehicle Hire intranet page. 

Note that this PGPA section 60 blanket approval only applies where:  

a.  a provider is used under the WoAG arrangement (ie. Hertz); or 

AEC Procurement and Contract Management Procedures 

Page 33 of 90


--- Page 34 ---

b.  another provider is engaged and their standard terms and conditions have been reviewed by CLP 

and legal advice provided; and 
the risks of hire are adequately addressed in the risk assessment attached to the blanket approval. 

c. 

For the avoidance of doubt, if an AEC official determines that the risks of hire (including through Hertz 
under the WoAG arrangement) are not adequately addressed in the standard risk assessment, a new risk 
assessment and PGPA section 60 approval will need to be undertaken separately. 

Other points to note when hiring vehicles 
PGPA Rule 18 approval is required prior to the hire of a vehicle from any provider (Hertz or alternative 
provider).  

The email for financial delegate approvals under $10,000 can be used for Hertz vehicle hire, or alternative 
provider vehicle hire where the cost is less than $10,000 (GST inclusive) and the purchase will be made 
by an AEC Corporate Credit Card.  

All other vehicle hire (of any value) from an alternative provider must be entered into My 
Procurement and Rule 18 approval obtained through the Spending Proposal (Stage 2) process.  

Questions about the travel policy should be directed to AEC Travel.
